The Definitive Superiority of Abu Bakr
Author defends the definitive superiority of Abu Bakr and clarifies the meaning of 'superiority' in the earlier discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that the superiority of Abu Bakr over the other companions is definitively proven by the Prophet's appointment of him to lead prayer.

Core Idea
The Prophet's choice of Abu Bakr to lead prayer proves his superiority definitively.

In this passage, the author clears up a common misunderstanding about the ranking of the caliphs after the Prophet. He then presents a powerful textual proof from the Sunnah that establishes Abu Bakr's superiority as a certain (qatʿī) matter. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The author first clarifies that his earlier mention of 'hesitation' referred specifically to the comparison between Uthman and Ali, not to the overall ranking of the four caliphs. Note how he corrects the misunderstanding of some commentators.
This sets the stage for his main argument.
2
Identify the author's core claim
The author declares that the preference of Abu Bakr is definitive (qatʿī). Look for the phrase 'هذا والذي أعتقده' — he is stating his own firm belief.
3
Examine the proof from prayer leadership
The proof is the Prophet's order for Abu Bakr to lead the prayer in his place, while Ali and other senior companions were present. The author connects this to the principle that the one most deserving of leadership is the best.
Also note the incident where Umar was prevented from leading.
4
Reflect on the reasoning
Ask yourself: Why does the author consider this proof definitive? How does it rule out any doubt about Abu Bakr's superiority?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومراده بالأفضلية أفضلية عثمان على علي رضي الله عنهما بقرينة ما قبله من ذكر التوقف فيما بينهما، لا الأفضلية بين الأربعة كما فهم أكثر المحشين حيث قال بعضهم بعد قوله فلا لأنه لا يكاد يخفى أن تقديم علي رضي الله عنه على الشيخين مخالف لمذهب أهل السنة والجماعة على ما عليه جميع السلف. وإنما ذهب بعض الخلف إلى تفضيل علي رضي الله عنه على عثمان رضي الله عنه، ومنهم أبو طفيل من الصحابة رضي الله عنهم. هذا والذي أعتقده وفي دين أعتمده أن تفضيل أبي بكر رضي الله عنه قطعي حيث أمره رسول الله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم بالإمامة على طريق النيابة مع أن المعلوم من الدين أن الأولى بالإمامة أفضل، وقد كان علي كرم وجهه حاضرا في المدينة، وكذا غيره من أكابر الصحابة رضي الله عنهم، وعينه عليه الصلاة والسلام لما علم أنه أفضل الأنام في تلك الأيام، حتى إنه تأخر مرة وتقدم عمر رضي الله عنه، فقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «إن هذا أمر لا ينبغي أن يتقدم فيه إلا أبو بكر».
And his intention by 'superiority' is the superiority of Uthman over Ali, may Allah be pleased with them, by the indication of what preceded it of mention of hesitation between them, not the superiority among the four as most of the marginal commentators understood, where one of them said after his statement 'then no' because it is no secret that preferring Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, over the two shaykhs contradicts the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah upon what all the Salaf were. Rather, some of the later scholars went to preferring Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, over Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, among them Abu Tufayl from the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them. This is what I believe and in my religion I rely upon: that the preference of Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, is definitive because the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, ordered him to lead the prayer by way of deputyship, while it is known from the religion that the one who is foremost for leadership is best. And Ali, may Allah honor his face, was present in Medina, as were others from the senior Companions, may Allah be pleased with them. And he, peace and blessings be upon him, appointed him because he knew that he was the best of people in those days, such that one time he was delayed and Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, stepped forward, so he, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'Indeed this is a matter in which none should step forward except Abu Bakr.'
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage clarifies the intended meaning of 'superiority' (al-afdaliyya) in the previous discussion. The author explains that the earlier mention of hesitation referred specifically to the preference of Uthman over Ali, not the overall order among all four caliphs, as some marginal commentators (al-muhashshin) misunderstood. The author then strongly asserts that preferring Ali over the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar) contradicts the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah and the way of all the Salaf. However, he notes that some later scholars, including the Companion Abu Tufayl, did prefer Ali over Uthman specifically. The author then declares his own belief: the preference of Abu Bakr is definitive (qat'i), because the Prophet appointed him to lead the prayer (imama) by deputyship while Ali and other senior Companions were present. Since the principle is that the one most deserving of leadership is the best, this indicates Abu Bakr's superiority. The author recounts an incident where Umar once stepped forward to lead prayer and the Prophet intervened, saying that none should step forward except Abu Bakr, reinforcing that Abu Bakr was the designated leader. Thus, the author defends the definitive superiority of Abu Bakr based on textual evidence.

الشيخان
إمامة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the superiority of Abu Bakr is a matter of personal opinion or ijtihad, and there is no definitive proof.
Correction: The author argues that Abu Bakr's superiority is definitive (qatʿī) based on the Prophet's explicit appointment of him to lead prayer, which is a clear textual evidence.

Checkpoint
What does the author present as definitive proof for the superiority of Abu Bakr?
A) A consensus of the people of Medina.
B) The Prophet's appointment of him to lead prayer in his place.
C) A direct revelation naming him as caliph.
D) His age and seniority.

Key Takeaway
The author asserts that Abu Bakr's superiority is definitive because the Prophet appointed him to lead prayer, which is a clear textual indicator of his excellence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you genuinely accept, in the depths of your heart, that Abu Bakr's superiority over all other Companions is a definitive matter of faith, or do you find yourself entertaining doubts that contradict the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna? If so, what is holding you back from full submission to this prophetic evidence?
?
If you had been present in Medina when the Prophet chose Abu Bakr to lead the prayer while Ali and others were there, would your own ego have tempted you to question that choice? How does that hypothetical reveal the state of your trust in the Prophet's decisions?
?
The Prophet's public correction of Umar — 'none should step forward except Abu Bakr' — shows that even the most virtuous can be mistaken about ranks. Where in your own life do you need to realign your judgments of people's worth with what Allah and His Messenger have established, rather than your own preferences?

Refuting Denial of Certainty in Companion Preference
Author refutes the denial of certainty in companion preference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over certainty of preference among the Companions, the arguments against it, the defense of Ali's superiority, and the author's refutation of the charge of Rafd.

Core Idea
Certainty of Ali's virtue is defended through mutawātir reports, not sectarian bias.

This passage presents a critical exchange on whether we can be certain about the superiority of one Companion over another. Some scholars argued that shared virtues make certainty impossible, but a commentator counters that Ali's merits are so numerous and widely transmitted that denial is impossible. The author then defends this view from the charge of Rafd, warning against bigotry.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument against certainty
Read the first part of the passage where some later scholars claim that no virtue is exclusive to one Companion, so certainty of preference is impossible.
Notice the reasoning: every virtue is shared, or a single virtue might outweigh many.
2
See the counterargument for certainty
Read the commentator's response that there is no room for hesitation; one must be certain of Ali's superiority due to the abundant and mutawātir (recurrently transmitted) virtues.
3
Note the accusation and refutation
The author mentions that this view was accused of having a whiff of Rafd, but he declares it a slander. Identify the reasons he gives for dismissing the accusation.
The author argues that Ali's virtues are undeniable and that if this were Rafd, no Sunni would exist.
4
Reflect on the lesson's warning
The author concludes with a warning against bigotry in religion and turning away from certain truth. Consider how this applies to discussions about the Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والمنقول عن بعض المتأخرين أنه لا جزم بالأفضلية بهذا المعنى أيضاً، إذ ما من فضيلة تروى لأحدهم إلا ولغيره مشاركة فيها وبتقدير اختصاصها به حقيقة فقد يوجد لغيره أيضاً اختصاصه بغيرها، على أنه يمكن أن يكون فضيلة واحدة أرجح من فضائل كثيرة إما لشرفها في نفسها أو لزيادة كميتها. وقال محش :آخر أي فلا جهة للتوقف بل يجب أن يجزم بأفضلية علي رضي الله عنه ، إذ قد تواتر في حقه ما يدل على عموم مناقبه ووفور فضائله واتصافه بالكمالات واختصاصه بالكرامات هذا هو المفهوم من سوق كلامه، ولذا قيل فيه رائحة من الرفض، لكنه فرية بلا مرية؛ إذ كثرة فضائل علي رضي الله عنه وكمالاته العلية وتواتر النقل فيه معنى بحيث لا يمكن لأحد إنكاره، ولو كان هذا رفضا وتركا للسنة لم يوجد من أهل الرواية والدراية سني أصلا فإياك والتعصب في الدين والتجنب عن الحق اليقين انتهى.
He said: And what is transmitted from some later [scholars] is that there is no certainty regarding preference in this sense either, because there is no virtue narrated for any one of them except that another has a share in it, and even if it is estimated that it is exclusive to him in reality, there may exist for another also exclusivity in something else, and it is possible that a single virtue may outweigh many virtues either because of its honor in itself or due to the increase of its quantity. And another commentator said: i.e., there is no room for hesitation; rather, one must be certain of the superiority of Ali, may God be pleased with him, for what indicates the generality of his merits, abundance of his virtues, his description with perfections, and his exclusivity with miracles has been recurrently transmitted regarding him. This is what is understood from the context of his speech, and thus it was said that there is a whiff of Rafd in it, but it is a slander without doubt; for the abundance of Ali’s virtues and lofty perfections and the recurrent transmission regarding him in meaning is such that no one can deny it. And if this were Rafd and abandonment of the Sunna, there would be no Sunni among the people of transmission and knowledge at all. So beware of bigotry in religion and of turning away from certain truth. End.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage reports a scholarly exchange on the certainty of preference among the Companions. First, it states that some later scholars hold that one cannot be certain of the best Companion because every virtue is shared, and a single virtue may outweigh many others. Then a commentator (muhashshin) argues that there is no room for hesitation and one must be certain of Ali's superiority due to the abundant and widely transmitted (mutawātir) merits that indicate his perfections and miracles. The author then comments that this view has been accused of having a whiff of Rafd (rejection of the first two caliphs), but he declares this accusation a clear slander, because Ali's virtues are undeniable. He argues that if acknowledging these virtues were Rafd, then no Sunni would exist among the scholars. He concludes by warning against bigotry in religion and turning away from certain truth.

Key Terms
فضلية
أفضلية
تواتر
رفض
فرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage asserts that Ali is definitively superior to Abu Bakr.
Correction: The passage does not compare Ali to Abu Bakr; it only defends the commentator's certainty of Ali's superiority against the charge of Rafd. The author does not specify that Ali is the best of all Companions, only that his virtues are certain.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given by some later scholars for denying certainty of preference among companions?
A) All virtues are shared.
B) The texts are contradictory.
C) Each companion is equal in virtue.
D) It is forbidden to speak about preference.

Key Takeaway
The passage defends the certainty of Ali's virtues through mutawātir transmission, rejects the accusation of Rafd, and warns against bigotry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a well-established truth about a Companion's virtue, do you accept it wholeheartedly, or do you hesitate due to sectarian biases? What would it cost you to embrace certain truth without prejudice?
?

?
Bigotry in religion blinds us to truth. Reflect on an area where you might be clinging to a position out of group loyalty rather than certain knowledge. Can you detach from that bias and commit to the truth alone?

Proof of Abu Bakr's Definitive Superiority
Author argues for the definitive superiority of Abu Bakr based on his imama and consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key arguments used by the author to establish the definitive superiority of Abu Bakr, including the Prophet's command for him to lead prayer and the subsequent consensus of the Companions.

Core Idea
The Prophet's command for Abu Bakr to lead prayer, while Ali was present, proves his definitive superiority.

This passage presents a central Sunni doctrine: the definitive superiority of Abu Bakr. The author combines two proofs: the Prophet's appointment of Abu Bakr as imam in prayer, which indicates his excellence, and the subsequent consensus of the Companions at Saqifa, which is protected from error. By studying this passage, you will see how the author constructs a categorical argument from these sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The author states that preferring Ali over the two shaykhs contradicts the Sunni madhhab. He notes that some later scholars preferred Ali over Uthman, but his own belief is that Abu Bakr's superiority is definitive.
2
Understand the primary proof
The Prophet commanded Abu Bakr to lead prayer while Ali and other senior Companions were present. The author argues that the one most entitled to lead prayer is the best, so this indicates Abu Bakr's superiority.
3
Examine the supporting hadith
The incident where Umar stepped forward and the Prophet said 'God and the believers refuse except Abu Bakr' reinforces the divine choice. Also, the incident with Aisha is mentioned.
4
Connect imamah to caliphate
The author asserts that this imamah was an indication of the caliphate. The Companions at Saqifa referenced it when choosing Abu Bakr as caliph.
5
Recognize the consensus argument
The Companions' consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate is a decisive proof because the Prophet said his community will not unite on error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يخفى أن تقديم علي رضي الله عنه على الشيخين مخالف لمذهب أهل السنة والجماعة على ما عليه جميع السلف. وإنما ذهب بعض الخلف إلى تفضيل علي رضي الله عنه على عثمان رضي الله عنه، ومنهم أبو طفيل من الصحابة رضي الله عنهم، هذا والذي أعتقده وفي دين أعتمده، أن تفضيل أبي بكر رضي الله عنه قطعي حيث أمره رسول الله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم بالإمامة على طريق النيابة مع أن المعلوم من الدين أن الأولى بالإمامة أفضل، وقد كان علي كرم وجهه حاضرا في المدينة، وكذا غيره من أكابر الصحابة رضي الله عنهم، وعينه عليه الصلاة والسلام لما علم أنه أفضل الأنام في تلك الأيام، حتى إنه تأخر مرة وتقدم عمر رضي الله عنه، فقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «أبى الله والمؤمنون إلا أبا بكر». وقضية معارضة عائشة رضي الله عنها في حق أبيها معروفة، وهذه الإمامة كانت إشارة إلى نصب الخلافة، ولذا قالت الصحابة رضي الله عنهم رضيه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم لديننا أو ما نرضى به في أمر دنيانا، وذلك حين اجتمعوا في سقيفة بني ساعدة واستقر رأيهم بعد المشاورة والمنازعة على خلافة أبي بكر رضي الله عنه، وإجماع الصحابة رضي الله عنهم حجة قاطعة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «لا تجتمع أمتي على الضلالة».
And it is not hidden that preferring Ali, may God be pleased with him, over the two shaykhs contradicts the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamaa'ah according to what all the Salaf were upon. Some later scholars went to preferring Ali over Uthman, may God be pleased with him, among them Abu Tufayl from the Companions, may God be pleased with them. This is what I believe and in my religion I rely upon: that the preference of Abu Bakr, may God be pleased with him, is definitive, because the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, commanded him with imamah in the way of deputyship, while it is known from the religion that the one most entitled to imamah is the best, and Ali—may his face be honored—was present in Medina, as well as others from the senior Companions, may God be pleased with them. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, designated him because he knew he was the best of creation in those days, even that once he (the Prophet) delayed and Umar stepped forward, so he, peace be upon him, said: 'God and the believers refuse except Abu Bakr.' And the incident of Aisha's objection, may God be pleased with her, regarding her father is well-known. This imamah was an indication of establishing the caliphate. That is why the Companions, may God be pleased with them, said: 'He, may God bless him and grant him peace, is pleased with him for our religion, so we are pleased with him for our worldly affairs.' That was when they gathered in the portico of Banu Sa'ida and their opinion was settled after consultation and dispute upon the caliphate of Abu Bakr, may God be pleased with him. And the consensus (ijma) of the Companions, may God be pleased with them, is a decisive proof due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'My community will not unite upon error.'
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the author's argument for the definitive superiority of Abu Bakr. First, he states that preferring Ali over Abu Bakr and Umar contradicts the Sunnī madhhab. He acknowledges that some later scholars prefer Ali over Uthman, but his own belief is that Abu Bakr's superiority is definitive (qaṭʿī). The proof: the Prophet appointed Abu Bakr to lead prayer (imamah) by way of deputyship, while knowing that the most excellent deserves the imamah. Ali and other senior Companions were present. The Prophet's saying 'God and the believers refuse except Abu Bakr' when Umar once stepped forward shows divine choice. This imamah pointed to the caliphate, leading the Companions at Saqifa to choose Abu Bakr. Their consensus is a decisive proof, as the Prophet said: 'My community will not unite upon error.'

Key Terms
إمامة
أفضلية
إجماع
صحابة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author relies solely on the hadith of 'God and the believers refuse except Abu Bakr' to prove Abu Bakr's superiority.
Correction: The author combines this hadith with the argument from the Prophet's command to lead prayer and the consensus of the Companions.

Checkpoint
What is the primary proof the author gives for the definitive superiority of Abu Bakr?
A) His wealth
B) His appointment as imam by the Prophet
C) His age
D) His lineage

Key Takeaway
The author proves Abu Bakr's definitive superiority through the Prophet's appointment as imam and the Companions' consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn that the Prophet appointed Abu Bakr to lead prayer while Ali was present, do you feel a tension between your personal sympathies and the clear evidence? Are you willing to submit your heart to what the texts prove, even if it challenges your emotional attachments? Make a firm intention to follow the truth wherever it leads, starting with this lesson.
?
The Companions at Saqifa said: 'He is pleased with him for our religion, so we are pleased with him for our worldly affairs.' Do you have that same level of trust in the choices of the Companions, or do you allow modern narratives to sow doubt in your heart? Examine your own faith: Do you truly believe that the generation chosen by Allah to accompany His Prophet could err in such a fundamental matter? Repent if you have harbored suspicion.
?
The author states that preferring Ali over Abu Bakr contradicts the way of the Salaf. Is there any area of your belief where you hold a view that contradicts the consensus of the early Muslims? Commit today to studying the positions of the Salaf and aligning your heart with them, no matter what popular opinion says.

Refuting the Claim of Taqiyya in Ali's Pledge
Author refutes Shi'a claim of taqiyya in Ali's pledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Shi'a claim of taqiyya regarding Ali's pledge to Abu Bakr is refuted.

Core Idea
Taqiyya is only known to the afflicted; one person's opposition does not break consensus.

The author addresses a key Shi'a claim that Ali's pledge to Abu Bakr was done under duress and dissimulation. He explains the real reason for Ali's delay and argues that the claim of taqiyya is baseless. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the background: Ali hesitated because of the Prophet's burial and grief, not reluctance.
2
Identify the Shi'a claim
The Shi'a claim Ali's action was taqiyya. Find the author's refutation.
3
Analyze the refutation
The author gives two reasons: taqiyya is known only to the person, and one person's dissent doesn't break consensus.
This is the core argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بايعه علي رضي الله عنه على رؤوس الأشهاد بعد توقف كان منه لعدم تفرغه قبل ذلك للنظر والاجتهاد، لما غشيه من الحزن والكآبة، ولما تعلق به أمر التجهيز والتكفين وإمضاء الوصية، فلما فرغ وتأمل في القضية دخل فيما دخل فيه الجماعة، وحمل الشيعة فعله على التقية مردود بأن التقية لم يطلع عليها إلا صاحب البلية، على أن مخالفة واحد ولو كانت ظاهرة لم تخرق إجماع الجماعة، إذ غايته أنه يدعي أو يزعم الأحقية من غير دليل أورده في القضية. ثم وقع الاتفاق على خلافة عمر رضي الله عنه، لكن تفضيله في زعمي أنه ظني إلا أنه قوي لم يختلف فيه سني، ويدل عليه كتابة الصديق رضي الله عنه على ما ذكر في "شرح المواقف": «هذا ما عهد أبو بكر بن أبي قحافة في آخر عهده من الدنيا وأول عهده بالعقبى حالة يبرّ فيها الفاجر ويؤمن فيها الكافر ، أني أستخلف عليكم عمر بن الخطاب، فإن أحسن السيرة فذاك ظني به والخير أردت، وإن تكن الأخرى فسيعلم الذين ظلموا أي منقلب ينقلبون».
And Ali, may God be pleased with him, pledged allegiance to him before the witnesses after a hesitation from him due to his lack of leisure before that for consideration and ijtihad, because of the sadness and depression that overcame him, and because the matter of preparation, shrouding, and executing the will was attached to him. When he finished and reflected on the matter, he entered into what the congregation entered into. The Shi'a interpreting his action as taqiyya (dissimulation) is rejected because taqiyya is only known by the one afflicted with it. Moreover, the opposition of one person, even if apparent, does not break the consensus of the congregation, since its utmost is that he claims or asserts entitlement without evidence brought in the matter. Then agreement occurred upon the caliphate of Umar, may God be pleased with him, but his preference in my opinion is speculative (ẓannī), yet it is strong; no Sunni disagrees on it. This is indicated by the letter of al-Siddiq, may God be pleased with him, as mentioned in 'Sharh al-Mawaqif': 'This is what Abu Bakr ibn Abi Quhafa has covenanted in his last days of this world and the first of his days of the Hereafter, in a state wherein the wicked is treated righteously and the disbeliever believes: indeed, I appoint over you Umar ibn al-Khattab. If he conducts well, that is my expectation of him and I intend good; if he does otherwise, then those who have wronged will know what turning point they will return to.'
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage addresses two issues. First, the Shi'a claim that Ali's pledge of allegiance to Abu Bakr was done under taqiyya (dissimulation). The author explains that Ali's delay was due to his preoccupation with the Prophet's burial and will, not from reluctance. After reflection, he joined the community. The claim of taqiyya is rejected because taqiyya is known only to the person in distress, and besides, one person's opposition does not break consensus. The author then discusses the caliphate of Umar. He states that Umar's superiority (over others) is speculative (ẓannī) but strong, and no Sunni disputes it. As evidence, he quotes the letter of Abu Bakr appointing Umar as his successor, which expresses hope for good conduct and warns against wrongdoing.

Key Terms
تقية
بيعة
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali's pledge was done under duress (taqiyya).
Correction: Ali delayed due to obligations and grief; after reflection he willingly joined the consensus.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the Shi'a claim that Ali's pledge to Abu Bakr was done under taqiyya?
A) By citing a hadith
B) By noting that taqiyya is only known to the person in distress
C) By saying Ali later complained
D) By denying the event

Key Takeaway
The claim of taqiyya is rejected because it is personal knowledge and one dissent does not break communal consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there times when you hide your true convictions to avoid conflict, claiming it's 'taqiyya' or just prudence? How does this passage's insistence that taqiyya is only known to the afflicted challenge your justification?
?
When you face a difficult decision, do you delay out of valid need or as an excuse to avoid commitment? How can you imitate Ali's eventual reflection and wholehearted joining of the community?
?
Think of a consensus in your community that you have privately questioned. Have you brought evidence for your view? If not, what does this lesson teach you about the weight of community agreement and the humility of lacking proof?

The Caliphate of Umar: A Strong Speculative Opinion
Author discusses Umar's caliphate and its speculative preference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of Umar's caliphate and why it is considered a strong speculative opinion.

Core Idea
Umar's superiority is speculative but strongly agreed upon by Sunnis.

After discussing Ali's pledge, the author turns to the caliphate of Umar. He clarifies that Umar's preference is not definitively certain, but a strong speculative opinion that all Sunnis accept. He cites Abu Bakr's letter as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Note the phrase 'tafdhiluhu fī zaʿmī annahu ẓannī illā annahu qawī' (his preference is speculative but strong).
2
Understand the evidence
The author quotes Abu Bakr's letter appointing Umar, which expresses hope and warning.
The letter shows Abu Bakr's expectation, not certainty.
3
Reflect on Sunni consensus
Despite being speculative, no Sunni differs on Umar's excellence.

Source Text

If he conducts well, that is my expectation of him and I intend good; if he does otherwise, then those who have wronged will know what turning point they will return to.'

?

?

Proof of Abu Bakr's Caliphate by Consensus
Sanusi cites consensus and a hadith to prove Abu Bakr's caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus of the Companions (ijma') serves as a decisive proof for Abu Bakr's caliphate, as established in the Saqifa incident and supported by a prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
The Companions' consensus is a definitive proof for Abu Bakr's caliphate.

This passage presents the Sunni argument for Abu Bakr's caliphate based on the historical consensus of the Companions at Saqifa. It also includes a prophetic guarantee that the community will not agree on error, making this consensus a binding proof. As you read, note how the author cites both a hadith and the historical event to establish legitimacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and historical context
Begin by reading the opening hadith and the description of the Saqifa gathering. Understand that the Companions explicitly preferred Abu Bakr for leadership.
The hadith 'Allah and the believers refused except Abu Bakr' is a key proof text.
2
Identify the claim of consensus
Notice that the passage states the Companions agreed after consultation and dispute. This agreement is presented as a unanimous decision.
3
Understand the proof from hadith
The author supports the consensus with the prophetic hadith 'My community will not agree on an error.' Reflect on why this makes the consensus conclusive.
This hadith is a foundational evidence for the authority of ijma' in Sunni Islam.
4
Connect to the lesson objective
Consider how the combination of historical event and prophetic guarantee forms a watertight argument for Abu Bakr's caliphate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه الصلاة والسلام: (أبى الله والمؤمنون إلا أبا بكر)) . (۱) وقضية معارضة عائشة رضي في حق أبيها معروفة، وهذه الإمامة كانت إشارة إلى نصب الخلافة، ولذا قالت الصحابة رضي الله عنهم رضيه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم لديننا أو ما نرضى به في أمر دنيانا، وذلك حين اجتمعوا في سقيفة بني ساعدة واستقر رأيهم بعد المشاورة والمنازعة على خلافة أبي بكر رضي الله عنه، وإجماع الصحابة رضي الله عنهم حجة قاطعة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: (لا تجتمع أمتي على الضلالة)).(۲) وقد بايعه علي رضي الله عنه على رؤوس الأشهاد بعد توقف كان منه لعدم تفرغه قبل ذلك للنظر والاجتهاد، لما غشيه من الحزن والكآبة، ولما تعلق به أمر التجهيز والتكفين وإمضاء الوصية، فلما فرغ وتأمل في القضية دخل فيما دخل فيه الجماعة، وحمل الشيعة فعله على التقية مردود بأن التقية لم يطلع عليها إلا صاحب البلية، على أن مخالفة واحد ولو كانت ظاهرة لم تخرق إجماع الجماعة، إذ غايته أنه يدعي أو يزعم الأحقية من غير دليل أورده في القضية.
Peace and blessings be upon him: 'Allah and the believers refused except Abu Bakr.' (1) And the matter of A'isha's opposition regarding her father is well-known. This imamah (leadership) was an indication for the appointment of the caliphate. Therefore the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) said: 'We accept for our religion what the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) accepted, or what we approve for our worldly affairs.' That was when they gathered in the portico of Banu Sa'ida, and their opinion settled after consultation and dispute on the caliphate of Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him). The consensus (ijma') of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) is a conclusive proof because of his statement (peace and blessings be upon him): 'My community will not agree on an error.' (2) And 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) gave him the pledge of allegiance in front of witnesses after a hesitation from him due to his lack of prior availability for consideration and ijtihad, because of the grief and depression that overwhelmed him, and because he was occupied with the preparation, shrouding, and execution of the will. When he finished and contemplated the matter, he entered into what the group had entered. The Shi'a's interpretation of his action as taqiyya (dissimulation) is rejected because taqiyya is known only to the one afflicted. Moreover, the opposition of one person, even if apparent, does not break the consensus of the group, since its utmost is that he claims or asserts entitlement without any evidence he presented in the matter.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate and a refutation of a Shia objection. It begins by mentioning a hadith indicating divine preference for Abu Bakr. It then cites the consensus of the Companions in the portico of Banu Sa'ida as a decisive proof, supported by the prophetic hadith that the community will not agree on error. The passage then addresses the issue that 'Ali initially delayed his pledge, explaining it was due to his preoccupation with the Prophet's funeral and his own grief, and that once he considered the matter, he joined the consensus. The Shia claim that 'Ali acted under taqiyya is rebutted: taqiyya is known only to the one who practices it, and in any case, the opposition of a single person does not invalidate the consensus. The argument concludes that the dissenter merely claims rightful leadership without offering evidence.

Key Terms
إجماع
خلافة
سقيفة بني ساعدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary basis for the proof of Abu Bakr's caliphate in this passage?
A) A direct appointment by the Prophet
B) The consensus of the Companions
C) A will from the Prophet
D) The preference of the Prophet's family

Key Takeaway
The consensus of the Companions, supported by the prophetic guarantee of communal infallibility, establishes Abu Bakr's caliphate as a binding truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Companions set aside their personal opinions and disputes to unite behind Abu Bakr. Where in your life do you insist on your own view, even when trusted people around you have reached a consensus? What would it take for you to surrender your pride and join them?
?
The Prophet guaranteed that his community would never agree on error. Do you truly trust this promise when it challenges your own doubts or the opinions of modern critics? How would your life change if you submitted fully to the collective guidance of the early Muslims?
?
This lesson shows that legitimacy comes from the community's agreement, not just individual preference. When you make important decisions, do you seek the wisdom of others, or do you trust your own judgment alone? Reflect on a recent choice: did you consult others, and if not, why?

Refutation of the Taqiyya Objection
Sanusi refutes the taqiyya objection regarding Ali's pledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Shia claim that Ali acted under taqiyya when he pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr is rejected on rational and communal grounds.

Core Idea
Ali's delay was due to grief, not rejection, and taqiyya cannot break consensus.

This passage addresses a common Shia objection regarding Ali's initial delay in pledging allegiance to Abu Bakr. The author explains the real reasons for the delay and refutes the taqiyya explanation with two key arguments. As you read, pay attention to how the author dismantles the objection logically.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of Ali's delay
Read the explanation that Ali was occupied with the Prophet's funeral and overcome with grief, which prevented him from participating in the Saqifa decision.
This humanizes Ali and clarifies his absence was not due to opposition.
2
Identify the two refutation arguments
The passage gives two reasons to reject the taqiyya claim: 1) Taqiyya is known only to the practitioner, and 2) One person's opposition does not invalidate consensus.
These are analytical, not just rhetorical, rebuttals.
3
Reflect on the strength of the second argument
Consider that even if Ali had openly opposed, the consensus of the majority remains binding. The dissenter's claim lacks evidence.
This shows the robustness of the consensus proof.
4
Apply the lesson
Think about how this refutation defends the Sunni position that the caliphate was legitimate and that Ali eventually agreed with the majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه الصلاة والسلام: (أبى الله والمؤمنون إلا أبا بكر)) . (۱) وقضية معارضة عائشة رضي في حق أبيها معروفة، وهذه الإمامة كانت إشارة إلى نصب الخلافة، ولذا قالت الصحابة رضي الله عنهم رضيه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم لديننا أو ما نرضى به في أمر دنيانا، وذلك حين اجتمعوا في سقيفة بني ساعدة واستقر رأيهم بعد المشاورة والمنازعة على خلافة أبي بكر رضي الله عنه، وإجماع الصحابة رضي الله عنهم حجة قاطعة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: (لا تجتمع أمتي على الضلالة)).(۲) وقد بايعه علي رضي الله عنه على رؤوس الأشهاد بعد توقف كان منه لعدم تفرغه قبل ذلك للنظر والاجتهاد، لما غشيه من الحزن والكآبة، ولما تعلق به أمر التجهيز والتكفين وإمضاء الوصية، فلما فرغ وتأمل في القضية دخل فيما دخل فيه الجماعة، وحمل الشيعة فعله على التقية مردود بأن التقية لم يطلع عليها إلا صاحب البلية، على أن مخالفة واحد ولو كانت ظاهرة لم تخرق إجماع الجماعة، إذ غايته أنه يدعي أو يزعم الأحقية من غير دليل أورده في القضية.
Peace and blessings be upon him: 'Allah and the believers refused except Abu Bakr.' (1) And the matter of A'isha's opposition regarding her father is well-known. This imamah (leadership) was an indication for the appointment of the caliphate. Therefore the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) said: 'We accept for our religion what the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) accepted, or what we approve for our worldly affairs.' That was when they gathered in the portico of Banu Sa'ida, and their opinion settled after consultation and dispute on the caliphate of Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him). The consensus (ijma') of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) is a conclusive proof because of his statement (peace and blessings be upon him): 'My community will not agree on an error.' (2) And 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) gave him the pledge of allegiance in front of witnesses after a hesitation from him due to his lack of prior availability for consideration and ijtihad, because of the grief and depression that overwhelmed him, and because he was occupied with the preparation, shrouding, and execution of the will. When he finished and contemplated the matter, he entered into what the group had entered. The Shi'a's interpretation of his action as taqiyya (dissimulation) is rejected because taqiyya is known only to the one afflicted. Moreover, the opposition of one person, even if apparent, does not break the consensus of the group, since its utmost is that he claims or asserts entitlement without any evidence he presented in the matter.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate and a refutation of a Shia objection. It begins by mentioning a hadith indicating divine preference for Abu Bakr. It then cites the consensus of the Companions in the portico of Banu Sa'ida as a decisive proof, supported by the prophetic hadith that the community will not agree on error. The passage then addresses the issue that 'Ali initially delayed his pledge, explaining it was due to his preoccupation with the Prophet's funeral and his own grief, and that once he considered the matter, he joined the consensus. The Shia claim that 'Ali acted under taqiyya is rebutted: taqiyya is known only to the one who practices it, and in any case, the opposition of a single person does not invalidate the consensus. The argument concludes that the dissenter merely claims rightful leadership without offering evidence.

Key Terms
تقية
إجماع
خلافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Shia claim that Ali's delay in pledging allegiance was due to taqiyya, implying he secretly opposed Abu Bakr's caliphate.
Correction: Ali's delay was due to practical and emotional reasons (grief, funeral duties). Upon reflection, he joined the consensus. The taqiyya claim is baseless because it cannot be proven, and even if true, one person's opposition does not invalidate the consensus.

Checkpoint
What is the second argument used to refute the taqiyya claim?
A) Taqiyya is forbidden in Islam
B) The opposition of one person does not break consensus
C) Ali never practiced taqiyya
D) The hadith forbids dissimulation

Key Takeaway
The taqiyya objection is rejected because it is unverifiable and because one person's dissent, even if genuine, cannot overturn the established consensus of the Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever hid your true belief about a controversial truth because you feared rejection or harm? Does this lesson's insistence on evidence over internal states challenge you to be more courageous in your convictions?
?
If you were in Ali's position—grieving, overwhelmed, and under pressure—would you have had the integrity to research and then join the truth once you found it, even if it meant going against your initial inclination? What would your answer reveal about your current relationship with following the majority?
?
Sanusi says one person's opposition 'does not break the consensus' unless they bring proof. Is there a matter of faith where you are the one dissenting from the scholars—do you have proof, or are you merely comfortable in your disagreement? What would it mean for you to submit to the truth even if it costs you your independence?

The Appointment of Umar by Abu Bakr
Abu Bakr's covenant appointing Umar as caliph

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Abu Bakr's appointment of Umar was based on his ijtihad and that it is considered strong speculative evidence (zanni) accepted by all Sunnis.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's appointment of Umar is a strong speculative judgment accepted by all Sunnis.

The passage begins by addressing the legitimacy of Umar's caliphate. It explains that while the appointment was based on personal reasoning, it is a strong and universally accepted position among Sunnis. We then read Abu Bakr's actual covenant, which is evidence of this designation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to understand the document's wording.
Note the formal language of the covenant.
2
Identify the key term 'zanni'
The passage describes the preference (tafdil) of Umar as 'zanni' (speculative) but strong. Understand what this means in Islamic theology.
Zanni is contrasted with qat'i (certain).
3
Recognize the evidence
Abu Bakr's written covenant is presented as evidence. Notice how it serves as a proof for Umar's caliphate.
This is a historical document.
4
Understand Sunni consensus
The passage notes that no Sunni differs on Umar's caliphate. Reflect on why this unanimity is important.
Consensus (ijma') is a source of authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم وقع الاتفاق على خلافة عمر رضي الله عنه، لكن تفضيله في زعمي أنه ظني إلا أنه قوي لم يختلف فيه سني، ويدل عليه كتابة الصديق رضي الله عنه على ما ذكر في "شرح المواقف": «هذا ما عهد أبو بكر بن أبي قحافة في آخر عهده من الدنيا وأول عهده بالعقبى حالة يبرّ فيها الفاجر ويؤمن فيها الكافر ، أنى أستخلف عليكم عمر بن الخطاب، فإن أحسن السيرة فذاك ظني به والخير أردت، وإن تكن الأخرى فسيعلم الذين ظلموا أي منقلب ينقلبون». ثم استشهد عمر رضي الله عنه وترك الخلافة شورى بين ستة عثمان وعلي وعبد الرحمن بن عوف، وطلحة والزبير، وسعد بن أبي وقاص رضي الله عنهم، بمعنى أنهم يتشاورون فيما بينهم، ويعينون من هو أحق بها منهم بحسب رأيهم، وإنما جعلهم كذلك لأنه رآهم أفضل ممن عداهم وأحق بالخلافة ممن سواهم كما قال : مات رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو راض عنهم، إلا أنه لم يترجح في نظر عمر رضي الله عنه واحد منهم، فأراد أن يستظهر برأي غيره في التعيين، ولذا قال : إن انقسموا اثنين وأربعة فكونوا في الحزب الذي فيه عبد الرحمن، ثم فوّض الأمر خمستهم إلى عبد الرحمن ورضوا بحكمه، فاختار هو عثمان وبايعه بمحضر من عنهم، فبايعوه وانقادوا لأوامره وصلوا معه الجمع والأعياد، فكان إجماعا.
Then agreement occurred on the caliphate of Umar (may Allah be pleased with him). But its preference in my opinion is that it is speculative (zanni), yet it is strong; no Sunni differs on it. It is indicated by the document of al-Siddiq (may Allah be pleased with him) as mentioned in 'Sharh al-Mawaqif': 'This is what Abu Bakr ibn Abi Quhafa has covenanted at the end of his worldly life and the beginning of his life in the Hereafter, in a state wherein the wicked acts righteously and the disbeliever believes: I appoint over you Umar ibn al-Khattab. If he pursues a good course, that is my expectation of him and I desired good. If he does otherwise, then those who have done wrong will know to what outcome they will turn.' Then Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) was martyred and left the caliphate as a shura (consultation) among six: Uthman, Ali, Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, Talha, al-Zubayr, and Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas (may Allah be pleased with them), meaning that they would consult among themselves and appoint whomever they deemed most entitled by their opinion. He made them thus because he considered them superior to others and more entitled to the caliphate than anyone else, as he said: 'The Messenger of Allah (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) died pleased with them.' However, none among them was preponderant in Umar's view, so he wanted to seek support from another's opinion in the designation. Therefore he said: 'If they split into two and four, then be with the party that includes Abd al-Rahman.' Then he delegated the matter to Abd al-Rahman, and they all agreed to his judgment. He chose Uthman and gave him the pledge in their presence, so they pledged allegiance and obeyed his orders and prayed with him the Friday and Eid prayers. Thus it was a consensus.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a historical account proving the legitimacy of Umar and Uthman's caliphates. It begins by noting that the agreement on Umar's caliphate was based on the ijtihad of Abu Bakr, as documented in his covenant, and that this designation is strong (though speculative). It then describes Umar's method for selecting his successor: he appointed a council (shura) of six eminent Companions, whom he considered the best of the community and with whom the Prophet was pleased. He did not single out one because he did not see any one as clearly preponderant. He instructed that if the council splits, the majority (the group containing Abd al-Rahman) should be followed. Abd al-Rahman was delegated to choose, and he selected Uthman. The entire group then pledged allegiance to Uthman, yielding a consensus (ijma'), which is the ultimate proof of legitimacy.

Key Terms
تعيين
ظني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the nature of the preference (tafdil) for Umar's caliphate?
A) Certain (qat'i)
B) Speculative (zanni) but strong
C) Based on explicit text
D) Weak and disputed

Key Takeaway
Abu Bakr's appointment of Umar was a strong speculative judgment supported by a written covenant and accepted by all Sunnis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Abu Bakr wrote down his decision carefully, acknowledging it was speculative yet strong. When you make important decisions, do you document your reasoning and admit your uncertainty? How can you imitate his balanced approach of humility and action?
?
The covenant ends with a stark warning to wrongdoers. In what areas of your life might you be acting unjustly or failing to uphold trust? What step will you take this week to correct that?
?
The appointment of Umar was accepted by all Sunnis even though it was not based on a clear text. Are there matters of faith or practice you cling to that are not explicit, causing division? How can you learn from the consensus of the Companions?

The Shura of Umar and the Caliphate of Uthman
Umar's shura and the delegation to Abd al-Rahman leading to Uthman's selection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Umar set up a shura (consultative council) and how the consensus on Uthman's caliphate was achieved through delegation to Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf.

Core Idea
Umar's shura and delegation to Abd al-Rahman led to a consensus on Uthman's caliphate.

After Umar's martyrdom, he did not appoint a successor directly but left the matter to a council of six eminent Companions. The passage describes the process: Umar's instruction, the delegation to Abd al-Rahman, and the resulting consensus on Uthman. This demonstrates a legitimate method of leadership transition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the sequence of events.
2
Identify the six members of the shura
The passage lists Uthman, Ali, Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, Talha, al-Zubayr, and Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas. Note their status as the best of the community.
All were promised Paradise.
3
Understand the role of Abd al-Rahman
Umar instructed that if the council splits, the group containing Abd al-Rahman should be followed, and then he delegated the final decision to Abd al-Rahman alone.
This prevented deadlock.
4
See how consensus was formed
Abd al-Rahman chose Uthman, and all others pledged allegiance. The passage concludes that this was a consensus (ijma').
Consensus legitimizes the caliphate.

?

?

?

The Legitimacy of Ali's Caliphate
Sanusi affirms Ali's caliphate through consensus and a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Companions unanimously elected Ali as caliph and how a prophetic hadith confirms the validity of his caliphate.

Core Idea
Ali's caliphate was established by consensus and confirmed by prophecy.

After the martyrdom of Uthman, the senior Muhajirun and Ansar gathered and chose Ali as the caliph, recognizing him as the most excellent and entitled. This passage sets forth the grounds for his legitimacy, emphasizing the consensus and a prophetic proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the election
Read the Arabic text describing the consensus and plea to Ali. Reflect on the terms used for his virtue.
2
Note the key phrase 'بلا خلاف'
The author asserts that there is no dispute about the rightfulness of Ali's affair. This is a strong claim of legitimacy.
3
Consider the prophetic hadith
The hadith about thirty years of caliphate is presented as proof. Understand that Ali's caliphate falls within that period.
The hadith is incomplete in the source but its implication is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استشهد عثمان رضي الله عنه وترك الأمر مهملا ومجملا، فاجتمع أكابر المهاجرين والأنصار على علي كرم الله وجهه والتمسوا منه قبول الخلافة وبايعوه لما كان أفضل أهل عصره وأولاهم بالخلافة في دهره بلا خلاف في حقية أمره، وأما ما وقع من امتناع جماعة من الصحابة عن نصرة علي رضي الله عنه والخروج معه إلى المحاربة ومن محاربة طائفة منهم له كما في حرب الجمل وصفين فلا يدل على عدم صحة خلافته ولا على تضليل مخالفيه في ولايته، إذ لم يكن ذلك عن نزاع في حقيّة إمارته، بل كان عن خطأ في اجتهادهم حيث أنكروا عليه ترك القود من قتلة عثمان رضي الله عنه، بل زعم بعضهم أنه كان مائلا إلى قتله، والمخطئ في الاجتهاد لا يضلل ولا يفسق على ما عليه الاعتماد. ومما يدل على صحة خلافته دون خلافة غيره الحديث المشهور : ((الخلافة بعدي ثلاثون
Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) was martyred and left the matter neglected and ambiguous. Then the senior Muhajirun and Ansar gathered around 'Ali (may Allah honor his face) and asked him to accept the caliphate and gave him the pledge, because he was the best of his era and the most entitled to the caliphate in his time, without any dispute about the legitimacy of his affair. As for the fact that a group of Companions refrained from supporting 'Ali and going out to battle with him, and that a party among them fought him, as in the battles of the Camel and Siffin, that does not indicate the invalidity of his caliphate nor does it lead to declaring his opponents misguided regarding his governance. That was not due to a dispute about the legitimacy of his leadership, but rather due to an error in their ijtihad (independent reasoning). They objected to his failure to exact retaliation (qisas) for the killers of Uthman. Some even claimed that he was inclined to kill him. But the one who errs in ijtihad is not declared misguided nor sinful, according to the relied-upon view. Among the proofs of the validity of his caliphate, to the exclusion of others, is the well-known hadith: 'The caliphate after me will be thirty [years].'
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a defense of Ali's caliphate against objections arising from the battles of the Camel and Siffin. It begins by affirming that after Uthman's martyrdom, the leading Companions unanimously chose Ali as caliph due to his excellence and entitlement. The passage then addresses the fact that some Companions (like Aisha, Talha, al-Zubayr) opposed Ali militarily. It explains that this opposition does not invalidate Ali's caliphate nor lead to declaring those opponents as sinners or misguided. The cause was not a rejection of Ali's rightful leadership, but an error in ijtihad: they believed Ali should have immediately avenged Uthman's blood, and some even suspected him of involvement. The author asserts that an error in ijtihad does not entail sin or misguidance. Finally, a proof of Ali's caliphate is cited: the prophetic hadith that the caliphate after the Prophet would last thirty years, which corresponds to the collective reigns of the four rightly guided caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali), with Ali's being part of that era.

Key Terms
خلافة
أكابر المهاجرين والأنصار
أفضل أهل عصره
أولاهم بالخلافة
الحديث المشهور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ali's caliphate was widely disputed because of later battles.
Correction: The passage states that there was 'no dispute' about the rightfulness of his affair at the time of his election.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the Companions elect Ali as caliph?
A) He was the wealthiest
B) He was the best and most entitled
C) He forced them to
D) He was the only eligible candidate

Key Takeaway
Ali's caliphate was legitimate by consensus and confirmed by prophetic prophecy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about the disputes among the Companions after the Prophet's death, do you feel your heart settle with the truth that Ali's caliphate was established by consensus and prophecy, or do you harbor doubt? What would it take for you to fully submit to that fact and remove any trace of suspicion from your heart?
?
The Prophet foretold the exact duration of the caliphate thirty years. How does this precision in prophecy strengthen your conviction that every detail of Islamic history is under divine plan? What would change in your daily life if you truly lived with that certainty?
?
If you had been among the Companion at the time of Uthman's martyrdom, would you have had the courage to unite behind Ali despite the chaos? Examine your own character: are you one who unites behind the truth even when it is unpopular, or do you follow the crowd?

Sects and Disagreements
beginner
4 min
The Battles of the Camel and Siffin as Ijtihad Errors
Sanusi explains that the battles were due to ijtihad errors, not invalidity of caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the battles against Ali were due to ijtihad errors, not rejection of his caliphate, and that such errors do not make one sinful.

Core Idea
Opposing Ali in battle was an honest mistake, not a sin.

The author addresses the objection that some Companions fought Ali. He explains that their opposition was not a denial of his caliphate but a mistaken ijtihad regarding the retaliation for Uthman's killers. This lesson clarifies the important principle that an error in ijtihad does not entail sin or misguidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the battles
Read the Arabic text that mentions the opposition and fighting in the battles of Camel and Siffin. Notice that the author acknowledges these events.
2
Identify the reason for opposition
The passage states the reason: they objected to Ali's failure to exact retaliation for Uthman's killers, and some suspected his involvement.
3
Understand the principle of ijtihad error
The author asserts that an error in ijtihad does not make one misguided or sinful. This is a key theological principle that preserves the status of the Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استشهد عثمان رضي الله عنه وترك الأمر مهملا ومجملا، فاجتمع أكابر المهاجرين والأنصار على علي كرم الله وجهه والتمسوا منه قبول الخلافة وبايعوه لما كان أفضل أهل عصره وأولاهم بالخلافة في دهره بلا خلاف في حقية أمره، وأما ما وقع من امتناع جماعة من الصحابة عن نصرة علي رضي الله عنه والخروج معه إلى المحاربة ومن محاربة طائفة منهم له كما في حرب الجمل وصفين فلا يدل على عدم صحة خلافته ولا على تضليل مخالفيه في ولايته، إذ لم يكن ذلك عن نزاع في حقيّة إمارته، بل كان عن خطأ في اجتهادهم حيث أنكروا عليه ترك القود من قتلة عثمان رضي الله عنه، بل زعم بعضهم أنه كان مائلا إلى قتله، والمخطئ في الاجتهاد لا يضلل ولا يفسق على ما عليه الاعتماد. ومما يدل على صحة خلافته دون خلافة غيره الحديث المشهور : ((الخلافة بعدي ثلاثون
Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) was martyred and left the matter neglected and ambiguous. Then the senior Muhajirun and Ansar gathered around 'Ali (may Allah honor his face) and asked him to accept the caliphate and gave him the pledge, because he was the best of his era and the most entitled to the caliphate in his time, without any dispute about the legitimacy of his affair. As for the fact that a group of Companions refrained from supporting 'Ali and going out to battle with him, and that a party among them fought him, as in the battles of the Camel and Siffin, that does not indicate the invalidity of his caliphate nor does it lead to declaring his opponents misguided regarding his governance. That was not due to a dispute about the legitimacy of his leadership, but rather due to an error in their ijtihad (independent reasoning). They objected to his failure to exact retaliation (qisas) for the killers of Uthman. Some even claimed that he was inclined to kill him. But the one who errs in ijtihad is not declared misguided nor sinful, according to the relied-upon view. Among the proofs of the validity of his caliphate, to the exclusion of others, is the well-known hadith: 'The caliphate after me will be thirty [years].'
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a defense of Ali's caliphate against objections arising from the battles of the Camel and Siffin. It begins by affirming that after Uthman's martyrdom, the leading Companions unanimously chose Ali as caliph due to his excellence and entitlement. The passage then addresses the fact that some Companions (like Aisha, Talha, al-Zubayr) opposed Ali militarily. It explains that this opposition does not invalidate Ali's caliphate nor lead to declaring those opponents as sinners or misguided. The cause was not a rejection of Ali's rightful leadership, but an error in ijtihad: they believed Ali should have immediately avenged Uthman's blood, and some even suspected him of involvement. The author asserts that an error in ijtihad does not entail sin or misguidance. Finally, a proof of Ali's caliphate is cited: the prophetic hadith that the caliphate after the Prophet would last thirty years, which corresponds to the collective reigns of the four rightly guided caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali), with Ali's being part of that era.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The battles prove that Ali was not the rightful caliph and that those who fought him were sinful.
Correction: The battles were due to an ijtihad error about retaliation; they do not affect Ali's caliphate nor make the opponents sinful.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did some Companions oppose Ali in battle?
A) They thought he was not the caliph
B) They wanted a different caliph
C) They disagreed with his handling of Uthman's killers
D) They were following a different religion

Key Takeaway
Opposition to Ali in battle was an ijtihad error, not a sin or a rejection of his legitimate caliphate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been absolutely certain that someone else was wrong, only to later realize they had a legitimate reason? How does this passage challenge your tendency to judge others as sinful when they disagree with you? What concrete step can you take this week to withhold judgment and assume good faith?
?
If the Companions could make a mistake in ijtihad about such a grave matter as opposing the caliph, what does that say about your own certainty in your opinions? Are you willing to admit that your own 'ijtihad' might be wrong in matters where you feel strongly? What is one issue where you need to humble yourself before Allah and acknowledge you could be mistaken?
?
This lesson teaches that even when someone is clearly in error, you should not declare them misguided. Is there someone in your life you have written off as 'clearly wrong'? How does the principle that 'error in ijtihad does not make one sinful' change your view of that person? Will you reach out to them today or change your internal attitude towards them?
